{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01JFoY6PB1VVJUAjAoDRYDLn","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→We welcome contributions from the community. Please read the following guidelines carefully to\n     2→maximize the chances of your PR being merged.\n     3→\n     4→# Communication\n     5→\n     6→* Before starting work on a major feature, please reach out to us via GitHub, Slack,\n     7→  email, etc. We will make sure no one else is already working on it and ask you to open a\n     8→  GitHub issue.\n     9→* A \"major feature\" is defined as any change that is > 100 LOC altered (not including tests), or\n    10→  changes any user-facing behavior. We will use the GitHub issue to discuss the feature and come to\n    11→  agreement. This is to prevent your time being wasted, as well as ours. The GitHub review process\n    12→  for major features is also important so that [organizations with commit access](OWNERS.md) can\n    13→  come to agreement on design. If it is appropriate to write a design document, the document must\n    14→  be hosted either in the GitHub tracking issue, or linked to from the issue and hosted in a\n    15→  world-readable location.\n    16→* Specifically, if the goal is to add a new [extension](REPO_LAYOUT.md#sourceextensions-layout),\n    17→  please read the [extension policy](GOVERNANCE.md#extension-addition-policy).\n    18→* Small patches and bug fixes don't need prior communication.\n    19→\n    20→# Coding style\n    21→\n    22→* See [STYLE.md](STYLE.md)\n    23→\n    24→# Inclusive language policy\n    25→\n    26→The Envoy community has an explicit goal to be inclusive to all. As such, all PRs must adhere to the\n    27→following guidelines for all code, APIs, and documentation:\n    28→\n    29→* The following words and phrases are not allowed:\n    30→  * *Whitelist*: use allowlist instead.\n    31→  * *Blacklist*: use denylist or blocklist instead.\n    32→  * *Master*: use primary or main instead.\n    33→  * *Slave*: use secondary or replica instead.\n    34→* Documentation should be written in an inclusive style. The [Google developer\n    35→  documentation](https://developers.google.com/style/inclusive-documentation) contains an excellent\n    36→  reference on this topic.\n    37→* The above policy is not considered definitive and may be amended in the future as industry best\n    38→  practices evolve. Additional comments on this topic may be provided by maintainers during code\n    39→  review.\n    40→\n    41→# Breaking change policy\n    42→\n    43→Both API and implementation stability are important to Envoy. Since the API is consumed by clients\n    44→beyond Envoy, it has a distinct set of [versioning guidelines](api/API_VERSIONING.md). Below, we\n    45→articulate the Envoy implementation stability rules, which operate within the context of the API\n    46→versioning guidelines:\n    47→\n    48→* Features may be marked as deprecated in a given versioned API at any point in time, but this may\n    49→  only be done when a replacement implementation and configuration path is available in Envoy on\n    50→  main. Deprecators must implement a conversion from the deprecated configuration to the latest\n    51→  `vNalpha` (with the deprecated field) that Envoy uses internally. A field may be deprecated if\n    52→  this tool would be able to perform the conversion. For example, removing a field to describe\n    53→  HTTP/2 window settings is valid if a more comprehensive HTTP/2 protocol options field is being\n    54→  introduced to replace it. The PR author deprecating the old configuration is responsible for\n    55→  updating all tests and canonical configuration, or guarding them with the\n    56→  `DEPRECATED_FEATURE_TEST()` macro. This will be validated by the `bazel.compile_time_options`\n    57→  target, which will hard-fail when deprecated configuration is used. The majority of tests and\n    58→  configuration for a feature should be expressed in terms of the latest Envoy internal\n    59→  configuration (i.e. `vNalpha`), only a minimal number of tests necessary to validate configuration\n    60→  translation should be guarded via the `DEPRECATED_FEATURE_TEST()` macro.\n    61→* We will delete deprecated configuration across major API versions. E.g. a field marked deprecated\n    62→  in v2 will be removed in v3.\n    63→* Unless the community and Envoy maintainer team agrees on an exception, during the\n    64→  first release cycle after a feature has been deprecated, use of that feature\n    65→  will cause a logged warning, and incrementing the\n    66→  [runtime](https://www.envoyproxy.io/docs/envoy/latest/configuration/operations/runtime#statistics)\n    67→  `runtime.deprecated_feature_use` stat.\n    68→  During the second release cycle, use of the deprecated configuration will\n    69→  cause a configuration load failure, unless the feature in question is\n    70→  explicitly overridden in\n    71→  [runtime](https://www.envoyproxy.io/docs/envoy/latest/configuration/operations/runtime#using-runtime-overrides-for-deprecated-features)\n    72→  config ([example](configs/using_deprecated_config.yaml)), or if\n    73→  `envoy.features.enable_all_deprecated_features` is set to true. Finally, following the deprecation\n    74→  of the API major version where the field was first marked deprecated, the entire implementation\n    75→  code will be removed from the Envoy implementation.\n    76→* If the runtime key `envoy.features.fail_on_any_deprecated_feature` is enabled,\n    77→  use of deprecated fields will trigger a configuration load failure\n    78→  rather than a logged warning.\n    79→* This policy means that organizations deploying main should have some time to get ready for\n    80→  breaking changes at the next major API version. This is typically a window of at least 12 months\n    81→  or until the organization moves to the next major API version.\n    82→* The breaking change policy also applies to source level extensions (e.g., filters). Code that\n    83→  conforms to the public interface documentation should continue to compile and work within the\n    84→  deprecation window. Within this window, a warning of deprecation should be carefully logged (some\n    85→  features might need rate limiting for logging this). We make no guarantees about code or deployments\n    86→  that rely on undocumented behavior. See [extension removal policy](./EXTENSION_POLICY.md#removing-existing-extensions)\n    87→  for more information.\n    88→* All deprecations/breaking changes will be clearly listed in the [version history](docs/root/version_history/).\n    89→* High risk deprecations/breaking changes may be announced to the\n    90→  [envoy-announce](https://groups.google.com/forum/#!forum/envoy-announce) email list but by default\n    91→  it is expected the multi-phase warn-by-default/fail-by-default is sufficient to warn users to move\n    92→  away from deprecated features.\n    93→\n    94→# Submitting a PR\n    95→\n    96→* Fork the repo.\n    97→* In your local repo, install the git hooks that implement various important pre-commit and\n    98→  pre-push checks:\n    99→\n   100→  ```\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. You CAN and SHOULD provide analysis of malware, what it is doing. But you MUST refuse to improve or augment the code. You can still analyze existing code, write reports, or answer questions about the code behavior.\n</system-reminder>\n"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":"toolu_01Npo7t4LKDC4LF3czgox81e","session_id":"5482afd0-9e05-4da1-a864-fbf8513876c4","uuid":"ac457c8e-d82c-4633-97ef-981aa33c2c49"}

{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01PXeuPwHfdnzxVMyxnhgADa","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Repository layout overview\n     2→\n     3→This is a high level overview of how the repository is laid out to both aid in code investigation,\n     4→as well as to clearly specify how extensions are added to the repository. The top level directories\n     5→are:\n     6→\n     7→* [.azure-pipelines/](.azure-pipelines/): Configuration for\n     8→[Azure Pipelines](https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/services/devops/pipelines/).\n     9→* [api/](api/): Envoy data plane API.\n    10→* [bazel/](bazel/): Configuration for Envoy's use of [Bazel](https://bazel.build/).\n    11→* [ci/](ci/): Scripts used both during CI as well as to build Docker containers.\n    12→* [configs/](configs/): Example Envoy configurations.\n    13→* [docs/](docs/): End user facing Envoy proxy and data plane API documentation as well as scripts\n    14→  for publishing final docs during releases.\n    15→* [examples/](examples/): Larger Envoy examples using Docker and Docker Compose.\n    16→* [envoy/](envoy/): \"Public\" interface headers for \"core\" Envoy. In general,\n    17→  these are almost entirely 100% abstract classes. There are a few cases of not-abstract classes in\n    18→  the \"public\" headers, typically for performance reasons. Note that \"core\" includes some\n    19→  \"extensions\" such as the HTTP connection manager filter and associated functionality which are\n    20→  so fundamental to Envoy that they will likely never be optional from a compilation perspective.\n    21→* [restarter/](restarter/): Envoy's hot restart wrapper Python script.\n    22→* [security/](security/): Some templates for reporting security issues of Envoy. Historical security issues can also be found here.\n    23→* [source/](source/): Source code for core Envoy as well as extensions. The layout of this directory\n    24→  is discussed in further detail below.\n    25→* [support/](support/): Development support scripts (pre-commit Git hooks, etc.)\n    26→* [test/](test/): Test code for core Envoy as well as extensions. The layout of this directory is\n    27→  discussed in further detail below.\n    28→* [tools/](tools/): Miscellaneous tools that have not found a home somewhere else.\n    29→\n    30→## [source/](source/)\n    31→\n    32→* [common/](source/common/): Core Envoy code (not specific to extensions) that is also not\n    33→  specific to a standalone server implementation. I.e., this is the code that could be used if Envoy\n    34→  were eventually embedded as a library.\n    35→* [docs/](source/docs/): Miscellaneous developer/design documentation that is not relevant for\n    36→  the public user documentation.\n    37→* [exe/](source/exe/): Code specific to building the final production Envoy server binary. This is\n    38→  the only code that is not shared by integration and unit tests.\n    39→* [extensions/](source/extensions/): Extensions to the core Envoy code. The layout of this\n    40→  directory is discussed in further detail below.\n    41→* [server/](source/server/): Code specific to running Envoy as a standalone server. E.g,\n    42→  configuration, server startup, workers, etc. Over time, the line between `common/` and `server/`\n    43→  has become somewhat blurred. Use best judgment as to where to place something.\n    44→\n    45→## [test/](test/)\n    46→\n    47→Not every directory within test is described below, but a few highlights:\n    48→\n    49→* Unit tests are found in directories matching their [source/](source/) equivalents. E.g.,\n    50→  [common/](test/common/), [exe/](test/exe/), and [server/](test/server/).\n    51→* Extension unit tests also match their source equivalents in [extensions/](test/extensions/).\n    52→* [integration/](test/integration/) holds end-to-end integration tests using roughly the real\n    53→  Envoy server code, fake downstream clients, and fake upstream servers. Integration tests also\n    54→  test some of the extensions found in the repository. Note that in the future, we would like to\n    55→  allow integration tests that are specific to extensions and are not required for covering\n    56→  \"core\" Envoy functionality. Those integration tests will likely end up in the\n    57→  [extensions/](test/extensions/) directory but further work and thinking is required before\n    58→  we get to that point.\n    59→* [mocks/](test/mocks/) contains mock implementations of all of the core Envoy interfaces found in\n    60→  [include/](include/).\n    61→* Other directories include tooling used for configuration testing, coverage testing, fuzz testing,\n    62→  common test code, etc.\n    63→\n    64→## [source/extensions](source/extensions/) layout\n    65→\n    66→We maintain a very specific code and namespace layout for extensions. This aids in discovering\n    67→code/extensions, and allows us specify extension owners in [CODEOWNERS](CODEOWNERS).\n    68→\n    69→* All extensions are either registered in [all_extensions.bzl](source/extensions/all_extensions.bzl)\n    70→  or [extensions_build_config.bzl](source/extensions/extensions_build_config.bzl). The former is\n    71→  for extensions that cannot be removed from the primary Envoy build. The latter is for extensions\n    72→  that can be removed on a site specific basis. See [bazel/README.md](bazel/README.md) for how to\n    73→  compile out extensions on a site specific basis. Note that by default extensions should be\n    74→  removable from the build unless there is a very good reason.\n    75→* These are the top level extension directories and associated namespaces:\n    76→  * [access_loggers/](/source/extensions/access_loggers): Access log implementations which use\n    77→    the `Envoy::Extensions::AccessLoggers` namespace.\n    78→  * [bootstrap/](/source/extensions/bootstrap): Bootstrap extensions which use\n    79→    the `Envoy::Extensions::Bootstrap` namespace.\n    80→  * [clusters/](/source/extensions/clusters): Cluster extensions which use the\n    81→    `Envoy::Extensions::Clusters` namespace.\n    82→  * [compression/](/source/extensions/compression): Compression extensions\n    83→    which use `Envoy::Extensions::Compression` namespace.\n    84→  * [fatal_actions/](/source/extensions/fatal_actions): Fatal Action extensions\n    85→    which use the `Envoy::Extensions::FatalActions` namespace.\n    86→  * [filters/http/](/source/extensions/filters/http): HTTP L7 filters which use the\n    87→    `Envoy::Extensions::HttpFilters` namespace.\n    88→  * [filters/listener/](/source/extensions/filters/listener): Listener filters which use the\n    89→    `Envoy::Extensions::ListenerFilters` namespace.\n    90→  * [filters/network/](/source/extensions/filters/network): L4 network filters which use the\n    91→    `Envoy::Extensions::NetworkFilters` namespace.\n    92→  * [formatters/](/source/extensions/formatters): Access log formatters which use the\n    93→    `Envoy::Extensions::Formatters` namespace.\n    94→  * [grpc_credentials/](/source/extensions/grpc_credentials): Custom gRPC credentials which use the\n    95→    `Envoy::Extensions::GrpcCredentials` namespace.\n    96→  * [health_checker/](/source/extensions/health_checker): Custom health checkers which use the\n    97→    `Envoy::Extensions::HealthCheckers` namespace.\n    98→  * [internal_redirect/](/source/extensions/internal_redirect): Internal Redirect\n    99→    extensions which use the `Envoy::Extensions::InternalRedirect` namespace.\n   100→  * [quic_listeners/](/source/extensions/quic_listeners): QUIC extensions which\n   101→    use the `Envoy::Quic` namespace.\n   102→  * [resource_monitors/](/source/extensions/resource_monitors): Resource monitor\n   103→    extensions which use the `Envoy::Extensions::ResourceMonitors` namespace.\n   104→  * [retry/](/source/extensions/retry): Retry extensions which use the\n   105→    `Envoy::Extensions::Retry` namespace.\n   106→  * [stat_sinks/](/source/extensions/stat_sinks): Stat sink implementations which use the\n   107→    `Envoy::Extensions::StatSinks` namespace.\n   108→  * [tracers/](/source/extensions/tracers): Tracers which use the\n   109→    `Envoy::Extensions::Tracers` namespace.\n   110→  * [transport_sockets/](/source/extensions/transport_sockets): Transport socket implementations\n   111→    which use the `Envoy::Extensions::TransportSockets` namespace.\n   112→  * [upstreams/](/source/extensions/upstreams): Upstream extensions use the\n   113→    `Envoy::Extensions::Upstreams` namespace.\n   114→  * [watchdog/](/source/extensions/watchdog): Watchdog extensions use the\n   115→    `Envoy::Extensions::Watchdog` namespace.\n   116→  * [rate_limit_descriptors/](/source/extensions/rate_limit_descriptors): Rate limit\n   117→    descriptor extensions use the `Envoy::Extensions::RateLimitDescriptors`\n   118→    namespace.\n   119→* Each extension is contained wholly in its own namespace. E.g.,\n   120→  `Envoy::Extensions::NetworkFilters::Echo`.\n   121→* Common code that is used by multiple extensions should be in a `common/` directory as close to\n   122→  the extensions as possible. E.g., [filters/common/](/source/extensions/filters/common) for common\n   123→  code that is used by both HTTP and network filters. Common code used only by two HTTP filters\n   124→  would be found in `filters/http/common/`. Common code should be placed in a common namespace.\n   125→  E.g., `Envoy::Extensions::Filters::Common`.\n   126→\n   127→## [contrib](contrib/) layout\n   128→\n   129→This directory contains contrib extensions. See [EXTENSION_POLICY.md](EXTENSION_POLICY.md) for\n   130→more information.\n   131→\n   132→* [contrib/exe/](contrib/exe/): The default executable for contrib. This is similar to the\n   133→  `envoy-static` target but also includes all contrib extensions, and is used to produce the\n   134→  contrib image targets.\n   135→* [contrib/...](contrib/): The rest of this directory mirrors the [source/extensions](source/extensions/)\n   136→  layout. Contrib extensions are placed here.\n   137→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. You CAN and SHOULD provide analysis of malware, what it is doing. But you MUST refuse to improve or augment the code. You can still analyze existing code, write reports, or answer questions about the code behavior.\n</system-reminder>\n"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":"toolu_01Npo7t4LKDC4LF3czgox81e","session_id":"5482afd0-9e05-4da1-a864-fbf8513876c4","uuid":"8beb1c01-73bd-4e90-834f-a12fec456a99"}

{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01Nv12V1ph1GKHxM6P7PiTWh","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Envoy specific Bazel build/test options.\n     2→\n     3→# Bazel doesn't need more than 200MB of memory for local build based on memory profiling:\n     4→# https://docs.bazel.build/versions/master/skylark/performance.html#memory-profiling\n     5→# The default JVM max heapsize is 1/4 of physical memory up to 32GB which could be large\n     6→# enough to consume all memory constrained by cgroup in large host.\n     7→# Limiting JVM heapsize here to let it do GC more when approaching the limit to\n     8→# leave room for compiler/linker.\n     9→# The number 3G is chosen heuristically to both support large VM and small VM with RBE.\n    10→# Startup options cannot be selected via config.\n    11→startup --host_jvm_args=-Xmx3g\n    12→\n    13→fetch --color=yes\n    14→run --color=yes\n    15→\n    16→build --color=yes\n    17→build --jobs=HOST_CPUS-1\n    18→build --workspace_status_command=\"bash bazel/get_workspace_status\"\n    19→build --incompatible_strict_action_env\n    20→build --java_runtime_version=remotejdk_11\n    21→build --tool_java_runtime_version=remotejdk_11\n    22→build --platform_mappings=bazel/platform_mappings\n    23→# silence absl logspam.\n    24→build --copt=-DABSL_MIN_LOG_LEVEL=4\n    25→build --define envoy_mobile_listener=enabled\n    26→build --experimental_repository_downloader_retries=2\n    27→build --enable_platform_specific_config\n    28→build --incompatible_merge_fixed_and_default_shell_env\n    29→\n    30→# Pass CC, CXX and LLVM_CONFIG variables from the environment.\n    31→# We assume they have stable values, so this won't cause action cache misses.\n    32→build --action_env=CC --host_action_env=CC\n    33→build --action_env=CXX --host_action_env=CXX\n    34→build --action_env=LLVM_CONFIG --host_action_env=LLVM_CONFIG\n    35→# Do not pass through PATH however.\n    36→# It tends to have machine-specific values, such as dynamically created temp folders.\n    37→# This would make it impossible to share remote action cache hits among machines.\n    38→# build --action_env=PATH --host_action_env=PATH\n    39→# To make our own CI green, we do need that flag on Windows though.\n    40→build:windows --action_env=PATH --host_action_env=PATH\n    41→\n    42→# Allow stamped caches to bust when local filesystem changes.\n    43→# Requires setting `BAZEL_VOLATILE_DIRTY` in the env.\n    44→build --action_env=BAZEL_VOLATILE_DIRTY --host_action_env=BAZEL_VOLATILE_DIRTY\n    45→\n    46→build --test_summary=terse\n    47→\n    48→build:docs-ci --action_env=DOCS_RST_CHECK=1 --host_action_env=DOCS_RST_CHECK=1\n    49→\n    50→# TODO(keith): Remove once these 2 are the default\n    51→build --incompatible_config_setting_private_default_visibility\n    52→build --incompatible_enforce_config_setting_visibility\n    53→\n    54→test --test_verbose_timeout_warnings\n    55→test --experimental_ui_max_stdouterr_bytes=11712829 #default 1048576\n    56→\n    57→# Allow tags to influence execution requirements\n    58→common --experimental_allow_tags_propagation\n    59→\n    60→# Enable position independent code (this is the default on macOS and Windows)\n    61→# (Workaround for https://github.com/bazelbuild/rules_foreign_cc/issues/421)\n    62→build:linux --copt=-fdebug-types-section\n    63→build:linux --copt=-fPIC\n    64→build:linux --copt=-Wno-deprecated-declarations\n    65→build:linux --cxxopt=-std=c++20 --host_cxxopt=-std=c++20\n    66→build:linux --cxxopt=-fsized-deallocation --host_cxxopt=-fsized-deallocation\n {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_011it2P8TMigsMBxrJTN9pEr","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→## CI configuration\n     2→\n     3→CI is configured in .github/config.yml.\n     4→\n     5→The configuration is per-branch and in this way different branches can have a different\n     6→runtime configuration.\n     7→\n     8→In a pull request only 2 things are read from the config.yml submitted in the request:\n     9→\n    10→- version\n    11→- build image\n    12→\n    13→As these can change the way the CI runs they are allowed to change. No other configuration\n    14→is read from the pull request itself.\n    15→\n    16→### Checks\n    17→\n    18→Which checks should run against a commit or PR is configured under the `checks` key.\n    19→\n    20→The names of these checks should match any checks that are set to required for the repo,\n    21→and if a check is required this should be set in the config to ensure the check is marked\n    22→as skipped if the related runs are skipped.\n    23→\n    24→### Runs\n    25→\n    26→This controls which workflows run, and where necessary which jobs in the workflows.\n    27→\n    28→This paths can be configured with glob matches to match changed files.\n    29→\n    30→Paths are always matched for PRs.\n    31→\n    32→For push requests the config can be set to:\n    33→\n    34→- always (default): Always runs\n    35→- paths: Runs when paths match\n    36→- never: Doesnt run on pushes\n    37→\n    38→## CI requests\n    39→\n    40→### All CI is requested\n    41→\n    42→Whether triggered by push event or a pull_request all CI should be viewed as \"requested\".\n    43→\n    44→This is very important as it means we can treat incoming triggers in much the same way\n    45→as we might handle an incoming web request.\n    46→\n    47→Much like a web request, CI requests may be \"trusted\" or \"untrusted\" and as a consequence\n    48→have more or less capability or access.\n    49→\n    50→Again, much like web requests, CI requests cannot be assumed to be safe.\n    51→\n    52→Any incoming data - critically data over which a user has the capability to change should\n    53→be treated in the same way that user data is handled in a web request.\n    54→\n    55→Failure to do this opens our CI up to many of the same attacks you might expect in a web scenario\n    56→- mostly injection attacks of various sorts.\n    57→\n    58→### Requests are always made _from_ the triggering branch\n    59→\n    60→The only CI workflow that is required/used on any branch other than `main` is `request.yml`.\n    61→\n    62→This file contains any custom configurations required by the branch - for example, build images.\n    63→\n    64→The request workflow on any branch always delegates to the `_request.yml` on `main`.\n    65→\n    66→The `_request.yml` workflow contains all required configuration for handling an incoming request.\n    67→\n    68→All other CI listens for the request workflow to run, and then runs with the requested/parsed data.\n    69→\n    70→### CI is always run _in_ the context of main\n    71→\n    72→Other than updating configurations in any given `request.yml` - no CI workflows are parsed\n    73→anywhere other than in the context of `main`.\n    74→\n    75→This means that **all** changes must be made to the `main` workflows for _any_ branch _and_ for PRs.\n    76→\n    77→Like branch CI, PRs also run in the context of `main` - making changes to these files in a PR will have\n    78→no effect until/unless they are landed on the `main` branch.\n    79→\n    80→### Lifecycle of a CI request\n    81→\n    82→#### Incoming request:\n    83→\n    84→Requests can be triggered by a  `push` to `main` or a release branch or from a\n    85→`pull_request_target` to those branches.\n    86→\n    87→The `request.yml` file handles this and *must* live on every branch.\n    88→\n    89→This wf then calls the reusable `_request.yml` workflow, typically on `main`, but\n    90→branches can pin this if required.\n    91→\n    92→#### Request is handled by `_request.yml` workflow:\n    93→\n    94→This workflow initially reads the `.github/config.yml` from the target branch.\n    95→\n    96→It uses this to decide which CI and which checks need to be run, and collects information\n    97→about the CI request.\n    98→\n    99→This can be configured on a per-branch basis, by editing the file on the branch.\n   100→\n   101→This also holds the authoritative build image information.\n   102→\n   103→Users can request a CI run in a PR with custom build images by editing the config.yml file\n   104→on the relevant branch. CI will allow this but flag the change.\n   105→\n   106→Likewise the version is checked at this stage, and CI flags if it has changed.\n   107→\n   108→No other CI vars should be editable by users in a PR.\n   109→\n   110→#### CI check runs *on main* listen for incoming requests and run if required:\n   111→\n   112→These checks *always* run on `main` but with the repo checked out for the branch or the PR.\n   113→\n   114→If branches require custom CI this can be added in the relevant file *on main* with\n   115→a condition to only trigger for relevant target branch.\n   116→\n   117→#### Checks are completed at the end of each CI run:\n   118→\n   119→Currently this reports only on the overall outcome of the CI run and updates the check.\n   120→\n   121→We can add eg Slack reporting here to notify on failed `main` runs.\n   122→\n   123→#### Retesting\n   124→\n   125→PR CI can be retested by issuing `/retest` on the PR.\n   126→\n   127→This finds the checks related to the latest request and restarts them if they are\n   128→failed or cancelled.\n   129→\n   130→Links on the request page link to the original checks, but the checks themselves will\n   131→offer a `reload` button to refresh to the latest version.\n   132→\n   133→## Branch CI\n   134→\n   135→All CI is run on `main` - branch CI included.\n   136→\n   137→The CI will checkout the correct commits and run the CI at that point.\n   138→\n   139→This means that the CI on `main` should always be able to run the current supported branches.\n   140→\n   141→There are possible workaround for custom branch CI but the better path is to ensure legacy support\n   142→in current `main` or backport any required changes.\n   143→\n   144→## CI caching\n   145→\n   146→Currently only x86 Docker images are cached.\n   147→\n   148→Github has a hard per-repo limit of 10GB cache for CI which is LRU cycled when exceeded.\n   149→\n   150→This should just be enough to store x86 and arm Docker images for most of our release branches\n   151→but will not leave anything to spare.\n   152→\n   153→We can probably set up a bucket cache for bazel and other caching but this will need to be\n   154→done separately for un/trusted CI.\n   155→\n   156→### Cache mutex\n   157→\n   158→Due to shortcomings in Github's concurrency algorithm we are using a mutex lock that\n   159→is currently stored in the (private) https://github.com/envoyproxy/ci-mutex repository.\n   160→\n   161→The lock allows CI jobs to wait while the cache is being primed rather than all jobs attempting\n   162→to prime the cache simultaneously.\n   163→\n   164→## Development, testing and CI\n   165→\n   166→Any Github workflows that use the repository context (`pull_request_target`, `workflow_run`, etc)\n   167→**are not tested in Pull Requests**\n   168→\n   169→This means that changes to CI must be tested/verified in the (private) staging repository.\n   170→\n   171→### CI enabling vars\n   172→\n   173→The CI workflows and actions are receptive to certain environment variables being set.\n   174→\n   175→`ENVOY_CI`: this allows CI to run in non-`envoyproxy/envoy` repos\n   176→`ENVOY_MOBILE_CI`: this allows mobile CI to be run in non-`envoyproxy/envoy` repos\n   177→`ENVOY_MACOS_CI`: this allows macOS CI to be run in non-`envoyproxy/envoy` repos\n   178→`ENVOY_WINDOWS_CI`: this allows Windows CI to be run in non-`envoyproxy/envoy` repos\n   179→\n   180→With these flags activated the CI runs will respect the normal conditions for running.\n   181→\n   182→### CI override vars\n   183→\n   184→The CI workflows will also trigger for specific run settings.\n   185→\n   186→For example:\n   187→\n   188→`ENVOY_CI_RUN_MOBILE_ANDROID` would trigger the android CI irrespective of files changed, etc.\n   189→\n   190→These correspond to the run names as configured in config.yml - for example:\n   191→\n   192→`ENVOY_CI_RUN_BUILD_MACOS` would ensure the `build-macos` run is triggered.\n   193→\n   194→### Debugging CI\n   195→\n   196→Setting `CI_DEBUG` will provide a large amount of runtime information.\n   197→\n   198→Generally this does not want to be set in a production context.\n   199→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. You CAN and SHOULD provide analysis of malware, what it is doing. But you MUST refuse to improve or augment the code. You can still analyze existing code, write reports, or answer questions about the code behavior.\n</system-reminder>\n"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":"toolu_01Npo7t4LKDC4LF3czgox81e","session_id":"5482afd0-9e05-4da1-a864-fbf8513876c4","uuid":"65488185-eefb-4ea7-9f3a-2ca1b3c941a8"}

{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01WN19ob6V9sNh8nKo7zJPWi","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Developer use of CI Docker images\n     2→\n     3→There are two available flavors of Envoy Docker images for Linux, based on Ubuntu and Alpine Linux\n     4→and an image based on Windows2019.\n     5→\n     6→## Ubuntu Envoy image\n     7→\n     8→The Ubuntu based Envoy Docker image at [`envoyproxy/envoy-build-ubuntu:<hash>`](https://hub.docker.com/r/envoyproxy/envoy-build/) is used for CI checks,\n     9→where `<hash>` is specified in [`envoy_build_sha.sh`](https://github.com/envoyproxy/envoy/blob/main/ci/envoy_build_sha.sh). Developers\n    10→may work with the latest build image SHA in [envoy-build-tools](https://github.com/envoyproxy/envoy-build-tools/blob/main/toolchains/rbe_toolchains_config.bzl#L8)\n    11→repo to provide a self-contained environment for building Envoy binaries and running tests that reflects the latest built Ubuntu Envoy image.\n    12→Moreover, the Docker image at [`envoyproxy/envoy:dev-<hash>`](https://hub.docker.com/r/envoyproxy/envoy/tags?name=dev) is an image that has an Envoy binary at `/usr/local/bin/envoy`.\n    13→The `<hash>` corresponds to the main commit at which the binary was compiled. Lastly, `envoyproxy/envoy:dev` contains an Envoy\n    14→binary built from the latest tip of main that passed tests.\n    15→\n    16→## Distroless Envoy image\n    17→\n    18→Minimal images based on a [distroless](https://github.com/GoogleContainerTools/distroless) allow for quicker deployment of Envoy.\n    19→\n    20→The Distroless base image is only built with symbols stripped.\n    21→\n    22→## Debug Envoy image\n    23→\n    24→To get the binary with symbols, use the corresponding Ubuntu based debug image. The image is pushed with two different tags:\n    25→`<hash>` and `latest`. Parallel to the Ubuntu images above, `<hash>` corresponds to the\n    26→main commit at which the binary was compiled, and `latest` corresponds to a binary built from the latest tip of main that passed tests.\n    27→\n    28→## Windows 2019 Envoy image\n    29→\n    30→On August 31, 2023 the Envoy project ended official Windows support due to a lack of resources.\n    31→We will continue to accept patches related to the Windows build. Until further notice, Windows\n    32→builds are excluded from Envoy CI, as well as the Envoy release and security processes.\n    33→\n    34→The Windows 2019 based Envoy Docker image at [`envoyproxy/envoy-build-windows2019:<hash>`](https://hub.docker.com/r/envoyproxy/envoy-build-windows2019/)\n    35→is used for CI checks, where `<hash>` is specified in [`envoy_build_sha.sh`](https://github.com/envoyproxy/envoy/blob/main/ci/envoy_build_sha.sh).\n    36→Developers may work with the most recent `envoyproxy/envoy-build-windows2019` image to provide a self-contained environment for building Envoy binaries and\n    37→running tests that reflects the latest built Windows 2019 Envoy image.\n    38→\n    39→# Build image base and compiler versions\n    40→\n    41→Currently there are three build images for Linux and one for Windows:\n    42→\n    43→* `envoyproxy/envoy-build` &mdash; alias to `envoyproxy/envoy-build-ubuntu`.\n    44→* `envoyproxy/envoy-build-ubuntu` &mdash; based on Ubuntu 20.04 (Focal) with GCC 9 and Clang 14 compiler.\n    45→* `envoyproxy/envoy-build-centos` &mdash; based on CentOS 7 with GCC 9 and Clang 14 compiler, this image is experimental and not well tested.\n    46→* `envoyproxy/envoy-build-windows2019` &mdash; based on Windows ltsc2019 with VS 2019 Build Tools, as well as LLVM.\n    47→\n    48→The source for these images is located in the [envoyproxy/envoy-build-tools](https://github.com/envoyproxy/envoy-build-tools)\n    49→repository.\n    50→\n    51→We use the Clang compiler for all Linux CI runs with tests. We have an additional Linux CI run with GCC which builds binary only.\n    52→\n    53→# C++ standard library\n    54→\n    55→As of November 2019 after [#8859](https://github.com/envoyproxy/envoy/pull/8859) the official released binary is\n    56→[linked against libc++ on Linux](https://github.com/envoyproxy/envoy/blob/main/bazel/README.md#linking-against-libc-on-linux).\n    57→To override the C++ standard library in your build, set environment variable `ENVOY_STDLIB` to `libstdc++` or `libc++` and\n    58→run `./ci/do_ci.sh` as described below.\n    59→\n    60→# Building and running tests as a developer\n    61→\n    62→The `./ci/run_envoy_docker.sh` script can be used to set up a Docker container on Linux and Windows\n    63→to build an Envoy static binary and run tests.\n    64→\n    65→The build image defaults to `envoyproxy/envoy-build-ubuntu` on Linux and\n    66→`envoyproxy/envoy-build-windows2019` on Windows, but you can choose build image by setting\n    67→`IMAGE_NAME` in the environment.\n    68→\n    69→In case your setup is behind a proxy, set `http_proxy` and `https_proxy` to the proxy servers before\n    70→invoking the build.\n    71→\n    72→```bash\n    73→IMAGE_NAME=envoyproxy/envoy-build-ubuntu http_proxy=http://proxy.foo.com:8080 https_proxy=http://proxy.bar.com:8080 ./ci/run_envoy_docker.sh <build_script_args>\n    74→```\n    75→\n    76→Besides `http_proxy` and `https_proxy`, maybe you need to set `go_proxy` to replace the default GOPROXY in China.\n    77→\n    78→```bash\n    79→IMAGE_NAME=envoyproxy/envoy-build-ubuntu go_proxy=https://goproxy.cn,direct http_proxy=http://proxy.foo.com:8080 https_proxy=http://proxy.bar.com:8080 ./ci/run_envoy_docker.sh <build_script_args>\n    80→```\n    81→\n    82→To force the Envoy build image to be refreshed by Docker you can set `ENVOY_DOCKER_PULL=true`.\n    83→\n    84→```bash\n    85→ENVOY_DOCKER_PULL=true ./ci/run_envoy_docker.sh <build_script_args>\n    86→```\n    87→\n    88→\n    89→# Generating compile commands\n    90→\n    91→The `./ci/do_ci.sh` script provides a CI target that generates compile commands for clangd or other tools.\n    92→\n    93→```bash\n    94→ENVOY_GEN_COMPDB_OPTIONS=\"--vscode --exclude_contrib\" ./ci/do_ci.sh refresh_compdb\n    95→```\n    96→\n    97→## On Linux\n    98→\n    99→An example basic invocation to build a developer {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_016YTx9cMBe2YopxCRmDL4Hr","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Envoy tests\n     2→\n     3→Envoy uses the [Google Test framework](https://github.com/google/googletest) for\n     4→tests, including\n     5→[Google Mock](https://github.com/google/googletest/blob/master/googlemock/README.md) for\n     6→mocks and matchers. See the documentation on those pages for information on the\n     7→various classes, macros, and matchers that Envoy uses from those frameworks.\n     8→\n     9→## Integration tests\n    10→\n    11→Envoy contains an integration testing framework, for testing\n    12→downstream-Envoy-upstream communication.\n    13→[See the framework's README for more information.](https://github.com/envoyproxy/envoy/blob/main/test/integration/README.md)\n    14→\n    15→## Custom matchers\n    16→\n    17→Envoy includes some custom Google Mock matchers to make test expectation\n    18→statements simpler to write and easier to understand.\n    19→\n    20→### HeaderValueOf\n    21→\n    22→Tests that a HeaderMap argument contains exactly one header with the given key,\n    23→whose value satisfies the given expectation. The expectation can be a matcher,\n    24→or a string that the value should equal.\n    25→\n    26→Examples:\n    27→\n    28→```cpp\n    29→EXPECT_THAT(response->headers(), HeaderValueOf(Headers::get().Server, \"envoy\"));\n    30→```\n    31→\n    32→```cpp\n    33→using testing::HasSubstr;\n    34→EXPECT_THAT(request->headers(),\n    35→            HeaderValueOf(Headers::get().AcceptEncoding, HasSubstr(\"gzip\")));\n    36→```\n    37→\n    38→### HttpStatusIs\n    39→\n    40→Tests that a HeaderMap argument has the provided HTTP status code. The status\n    41→code can be passed in as a string or an integer.\n    42→\n    43→Example:\n    44→\n    45→```cpp\n    46→EXPECT_THAT(response->headers(), HttpStatusIs(\"200\"));\n    47→```\n    48→\n    49→### HeaderMapEqual and HeaderMapEqualRef\n    50→\n    51→`HeaderMapEqualRef` tests that two `HeaderMap` arguments are equal.\n    52→`HeaderMapEqual` is the same, but it compares two pointers to `HeaderMap`s, and\n    53→the matcher's argument must be a `HeaderMapPtr`.\n    54→\n    55→Example:\n    56→\n    57→```cpp\n    58→EXPECT_THAT(response->headers(), HeaderMapEqualRef(expected_headers));\n    59→```\n    60→\n    61→### ProtoEq, ProtoEqIgnoringField, RepeatedProtoEq\n    62→\n    63→Tests equality of protobufs, with a variant that ignores the value (including\n    64→presence) of a single named field. Another variant can be used to compare two\n    65→instances of Protobuf::RepeatedPtrField element-by-element.\n    66→\n    67→Example:\n    68→\n    69→```cpp\n    70→envoy::service::discovery::v3::DeltaDiscoveryRequest expected_request;\n    71→// (not shown: set some fields of expected_request...)\n    72→EXPECT_CALL(async_stream_, sendMessage(ProtoEqIgnoringField(expected_request, \"response_nonce\"), false));\n    73→\n    74→response->mutable_resources()->Add();\n    75→response->mutable_resources()->Add();\n    76→response->mutable_resources()->Add();\n    77→// (not shown: do something to populate those empty added items...)\n    78→EXPECT_CALL(callbacks_, onConfigUpdate(RepeatedProtoEq(response->resources()), version));\n    79→```\n    80→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. Download and extract source code of a release tarball from the Releases page. For example: https://github.com/envoyproxy/envoy/releases/tag/v1.24.0.\n    41→1. `python3 tools/github/write_current_source_version.py` from the repository root.\n    42→1. `bazel build -c opt envoy` from the repository root.\n    43→\n    44→> **Note**: If the the `write_current_source_version.py` script is missing from the extracted source code directory, you can download it from [here](https://raw.githubusercontent.com/envoyproxy/envoy/main/tools/github/write_current_source_version.py).\n    45→> This script is used to generate SOURCE_VERSION that is required by [`bazel/get_workspace_status`](./get_workspace_status) to \"stamp\" the binary in a non-git directory.\n    46→\n    47→> **Note**: To avoid rate-limiting by GitHub API, you can provide [a valid GitHub token](https://docs.github.com/en/authentication/keeping-your-account-and-data-secure/about-authentication-to-github#githubs-token-formats) to `GITHUB_TOKEN` environment variable.\n    48→> The environment variable name that holds the token can also be customized by setting `--github_api_token_env_name`.\n    49→> In a GitHub Actions workflow file, you can set this token from [`secrets.GITHUB_TOKEN`](https://docs.github.com/en/actions/security-guides/automatic-token-authentication#about-the-github_token-secret).\n    50→\n    51→Examples:\n    52→\n    53→```console\n    54→GITHUB_TOKEN=<GITHUB_TOKEN> python3 tools/github/write_current_source_version.py\n    55→MY_TOKEN=<GITHUB_TOKEN> python3 tools/github/write_current_source_version.py {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01CjdxoMBhqgDrp1m57QYa2L","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→When creating an Envoy pull request (PR) the text box will automatically be filled\n     2→in with the basic fields from the [pull request template](PULL_REQUEST_TEMPLATE.md). The following\n     3→is a more detailed explanation of what should go in each field.\n     4→\n     5→### <a name=\"title\"></a>Title\n     6→\n     7→The title of the PR should brief (one line) noting the subsystem or the aspect this PR applies to and\n     8→explaining the overall change. Both the component and the explanation must be lower case. For example:\n     9→\n    10→* ci: update build image to 44d539cb\n    11→* docs: fix indent, buffer: add copyOut() method\n    12→* router:add x-envoy-overloaded header\n    13→* tls: add support for specifying TLS session ticket keys\n    14→\n    15→### <a name=\"desc\"></a>Commit Message\n    16→\n    17→The commit message field should include an explanation of what this PR\n    18→does. This will be used as the final commit message that maintainers will use to\n    19→populate the commit message when merging. If this PR causes a change in behavior\n    20→it should document the behavior before and after. If fixing a bug, please\n    21→describe what the original issue is and how the change resolves it. If it is\n    22→configuration controlled, it should note how the feature is enabled etc...\n    23→\n    24→\n    25→### <a name=\"desc\"></a>Additional Description\n    26→\n    27→The additional description field should include information of what this PR does\n    28→that may be out of scope for a commit message. This could include additional\n    29→information or context useful to reviewers.\n    30→\n    31→### <a name=\"risk\"></a>Risk\n    32→\n    33→Risk Level is one of: Low | Medium | High\n    34→\n    35→Low: Small bug fix or small optional feature.\n    36→\n    37→Medium: New features that are not enabled(for example: new filter). Small-medium\n    38→features added to existing components(for example: modification to an existing\n    39→filter).\n    40→\n    41→High: Complicated changes such as flow control, rewrites of critical\n    42→components, etc.\n    43→\n    44→Note: The above is only a rough guide for choosing a level,\n    45→please ask if you have any concerns about the risk of the PR.\n    46→\n    47→### <a name=\"testing\"></a>Testing\n    48→\n    49→The testing section should include an explanation of what testing was done, for example: unit test,\n    50→integration, manual testing, etc.\n    51→\n    52→Note: It isn’t expected to do all forms of testing, please use your best judgement or ask for\n    53→guidance if you are unsure. A good rule of thumb is the riskier the change, the\n    54→more comprehensive the testing should be.\n    55→\n    56→### <a name=\"docs\"></a>Documentation\n    57→\n    58→If there are documentation changes, please include a brief description of what they are. Docs\n    59→changes may be in [docs/root](docs/root) and/or inline with the API protos. Please write in\n    60→N/A if there were no documentation changes.\n    61→\n    62→Any PRs with structural changes to the dataplane should also update the [Life of a\n    63→Request](https://www.envoyproxy.io/docs/envoy/latest/intro/life_of_a_request) documentation as appropriate.\n    64→\n    65→### <a name=\"relnotes\"></a>Release notes\n    66→\n    67→If this change is user impacting OR extension developer impacting (filter API, etc.) you **must**\n    68→add a release note to the [version history](changelogs/current.yaml) for the\n    69→current version. Please include any relevant links. Each release note should be prefixed with the\n    70→relevant subsystem in **alphabetical order** (see existing examples as a guide) and include links\n    71→to relevant parts of the documentation. Thank you! Please write in N/A if there are no release notes.\n    72→\n    73→### <a name=\"platform_specific_features\"></a>Platform Specific Features\n    74→\n    75→If this change involves any platform specific features (e.g. utilizing OS-specific socket options)\n    76→or only implements new features for a limited set of platforms (e.g. Linux amd64 only), please\n    77→include an explanation that addresses the reasoning behind this. Please also open a new tracking\n    78→issue for each platform this change is not implemented on (and link them in the PR) to enable\n    79→maintainers and contributors to triage. Reviewers will look for the change to avoid\n    80→`#ifdef <OSNAME>` and rather prefer feature guards to not enable the change on a given platform\n    81→using the build system.\n    82→\n    83→### <a name=\"runtime_guard\"></a>Runtime guard\n    84→\n    85→If this PR has a user-visible behavioral change, or otherwise falls under the\n    86→guidelines for runtime guarding in the [contributing doc](CONTRIBUTING.md)\n    87→it should have a runtime guard, which should be documented both in the release\n    88→notes and here in the PR description.\n    89→\n    90→For new feature additions guarded by configs, no-op refactors, docs changes etc.\n    91→this field can be disregarded and/or removed.\n    92→\n    93→### <a name=\"issues\"></a>Issues\n    94→\n    95→If this PR fixes an outstanding issue, please add a line of the form:\n    96→\n    97→Fixes #Issue\n    98→\n    99→This will result in the linked issue being automatically closed when the PR is\n   100→merged. If you want to associate an issue with a PR without closing the issue,\n   101→you may instead just tag the PR with the issue:\n   102→\n   103→\\#Issue\n   104→\n   105→### <a name=\"commit\"></a>Commit\n   106→\n   107→If this PR fixes or reverts a buggy commit, please add a line of the form:\n   108→\n   109→Fixes commit #PR\n   110→\n   111→or\n   112→\n   113→Fixes commit SHA\n   114→\n   115→This will allow automated tools to detect tainted commit ranges on the main branch when the PR is\n   116→merged.\n   117→\n   118→### <a name=\"deprecated\"></a>Deprecated\n   119→\n   120→If this PR deprecates existing Envoy APIs or code, it should include an update to the deprecated\n   121→section of the [version history](changelogs/current.yaml) and a one line note in the\n   122→PR description.\n   123→\n   124→If you mark existing APIs or code as deprecated, when the next release is cut, the\n   125→deprecation script will create and assign an issue to you for\n   126→cleaning up the deprecated code path.\n   127→\n   128→### <a name=\"api\"></a>API Changes\n   129→\n   130→If this PR changes anything in the [api tree](https://github.com/envoyproxy/envoy/tree/main/api),\n   131→please read the [API Review\n   132→Checklist](https://github.com/envoyproxy/envoy/tree/main/api/review_checklist.md)\n   133→and make sure that your changes have addressed all of the considerations listed there.\n   134→Any relevant considerations should be documented under \"API Considerations\" in the PR description.\n   135→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01PRtjMPxod35uEjBTyiggYx","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# C++ coding style\n     2→\n     3→* The Envoy source code is formatted using clang-format. Thus all white spaces, etc.\n     4→  issues are taken care of automatically. The Azure Pipelines will automatically check\n     5→  the code format and fail. There are make targets that can both check the format\n     6→  (check_format) as well as fix the code format for you (fix_format). Errors in\n     7→  .clang-tidy are enforced while other warnings are suggestions. Note that code and\n     8→  comment blocks designated `clang-format off` must be closed with `clang-format on`.\n     9→  To run these checks locally, see [Support Tools](support/README.md).\n    10→* Beyond code formatting, for the most part Envoy uses the\n    11→  [Google C++ style guidelines](https://google.github.io/styleguide/cppguide.html).\n    12→  The following section covers the major areas where we deviate from the Google\n    13→  guidelines.\n    14→\n    15→# Repository file layout\n    16→\n    17→* Please see [REPO_LAYOUT.md](REPO_LAYOUT.md).\n    18→\n    19→# Documentation\n    20→\n    21→* If you are modifying the data plane structurally, please keep the [Life of a\n    22→  Request](https://www.envoyproxy.io/docs/envoy/latest/intro/life_of_a_request) documentation up-to-date.\n    23→\n    24→# Deviations from Google C++ style guidelines\n    25→\n    26→* Exceptions are allowed on the control plane, though now discouraged in new code. Adding exceptions is disallowed on the data plane.\n    27→* References are always preferred over pointers when the reference cannot be null. This\n    28→  includes both const and non-const references.\n    29→* Function names should all use camel case starting with a lower case letter (e.g., `doFoo()`).\n    30→* Struct/Class member variables have a `_` postfix (e.g., `int foo_;`).\n    31→* Enum values using PascalCase (e.g., `RoundRobin`).\n    32→* 100 columns is the line limit.\n    33→* Use your GitHub name in TODO comments, e.g. `TODO(foobar): blah`.\n    34→* Smart pointers are type aliased:\n    35→  * `using FooPtr = std::unique_ptr<Foo>;`\n    36→  * `using BarSharedPtr = std::shared_ptr<Bar>;`\n    37→  * `using BlahConstSharedPtr = std::shared_ptr<const Blah>;`\n    38→  * Regular pointers (e.g. `int* foo`) should not be type aliased.\n    39→* `absl::optional<std::reference_wrapper<T>>` has a helper class in `envoy/common/optref.h`, and is type aliased:\n    40→  * `using FooOptRef = OptRef<T>;`\n    41→  * `using FooOptConstRef = OptRef<const T>;`\n    42→* If move semantics are intended, prefer specifying function arguments with `&&`.\n    43→  E.g., `void onHeaders(Http::HeaderMapPtr&& headers, ...)`. The rationale for this is that it\n    44→  forces the caller to specify `std::move(...)` or pass a temporary and makes the intention at\n    45→  the callsite clear. Otherwise, it's difficult to tell if a const reference is actually being\n    46→  passed to the called function. This is true even for `std::unique_ptr`.\n    47→* Prefer `unique_ptr` over `shared_ptr` wherever possible. `unique_ptr` makes ownership in\n    48→  production code easier to reason about. Note that this creates some test oddities where\n    49→  production code requires a `unique_ptr` but the test must still have access to the memory\n    50→  the production code is using (mock or otherwise). In these cases it is acceptable to allocate\n    51→  raw memory in a test and return it to the production code with the expectation that the\n    52→  production code will hold it in a `unique_ptr` and free it. Envoy uses the factory pattern\n    53→  quite a bit for these cases. (Search the code for \"factory\").\n    54→* Prefer explicitly sized integer types, such as uint64_t rather than size_t. In particular, use\n    55→  explicitly sized integers for data that is written to disk or involved in math that might overflow.\n    56→* The Google C++ style guide points out that [non-PoD static and global variables are forbidden](https://google.github.io/styleguide/cppguide.html#Static_and_Global_Variables).\n    57→  This _includes_ types such as `std::string`. We encourage the use of the\n    58→  advice in the [C++ FAQ on the static initialization\n    59→  fiasco](https://isocpp.org/wiki/faq/ctors#static-init-order-on-first-use) for\n    60→  how to best handle this.\n    61→* The Google C++ style guide points out that [constant vars should be named `kConstantVar`](https://google.github.io/styleguide/cppguide.html#Constant_Names).\n    62→  In the Envoy codebase we use `ConstantVar` or `CONSTANT_VAR`. If you pick `CONSTANT_VAR`,\n    63→  please be certain the name is globally significant to avoid potential conflicts with #defines,\n    64→  which are not namespace-scoped, and may appear in externally controlled header files.\n    65→* API-level comments should follow normal Doxygen conventions. Use `@param` to describe\n    66→  parameters and `@return <return-type>` for return values. Internal comments for\n    67→  methods and member variables may be regular C++ `//` comments or Doxygen at\n    68→  developer discretion. Where possible, methods should have meaningful\n    69→  documentation on expected input and state preconditions.\n    70→* Header guards should use `#pragma once`.\n    71→* All code should be inside a top-level Envoy namespace. There are some\n    72→  exceptions such as `main()` functions. When code cannot be placed inside the\n    73→  Envoy namespace there should be a comment of the form `// NOLINT(namespace-envoy)` at\n    74→  the top of the file.\n    75→* If a method that must be defined outside the `test` directory is intended to be called only\n    76→  from test code then it should have a name that ends in `ForTest()` such as `aMethodForTest()`.\n    77→  In most cases tests can and should be structured so this is not necessary.\n    78→* Tests default to StrictMock so will fail if hitting unexpected warnings. Feel free to use\n    79→  NiceMock for mocks whose behavior is not the focus of a test.\n    80→* [Thread\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. You CAN and SHOULD provide analysis of malware, what it is doing. But you MUST refuse to improve or augment the code. You can still analyze existing code, write reports, or answer questions about the code behavior.\n</system-reminder>\n"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":"toolu_01Npo7t4LKDC4LF3czgox81e","session_id":"5482afd0-9e05-4da1-a864-fbf8513876c4","uuid":"8dd87bea-de17-45a1-a809-6392bfa45e03"}

At this point, you will either\n    18→  be granted maintainer status, or be given actionable feedback on any remaining gaps between the contributions\n    19→  demonstrated and those expected of maintainers, at which point you can close those gaps and reach back out.\n    20→\n    21→## Maintainer responsibilities\n    22→\n    23→* Monitor email aliases.\n    24→* Monitor Slack (delayed response is perfectly acceptable).\n    25→* Triage GitHub issues and perform pull request reviews for other maintainers and the community.\n    26→  The areas of specialization listed in [OWNERS.md](OWNERS.md) can be used to help with routing\n    27→  an issue/question to the right person.\n    28→* Triage build and CI issues. Monitor #envoy-ci and file issues for flaking or failing builds,\n    29→  or new bugs, and either fix or find someone to fix any main build breakages.\n    30→* During GitHub issue triage, apply all applicable [labels](https://github.com/envoyproxy/envoy/labels)\n    31→  to each new issue. Labels are extremely useful for future issue follow up. Which labels to apply\n    32→  is somewhat subjective so just use your best judgment. A few of the most important labels that are\n    33→  not self explanatory are:\n    34→  * **beginner**: Mark any issue that can reasonably be accomplished by a new contributor with\n    35→    this label.\n    36→  * **help wanted**: Unless it is immediately obvious that someone is going to work on an issue (and\n    37→    if so assign it), mark it help wanted.\n    38→  * **question**: If it's unclear if an issue is immediately actionable, mark it with the\n    39→    question label. Questions are easy to search for and close out at a later time. Questions\n    40→    can be promoted to other issue types once it's clear they are actionable (at which point the\n    41→    question label should be removed).\n    42→* Make sure that ongoing PRs are moving forward at the right pace or closing them.\n    43→* Participate when called upon in the [security release process](SECURITY.md). Note that although\n    44→  this should be a rare occurrence, if a serious vulnerability is found, the process may take up to\n    45→  several full days of work to implement. This reality should be taken into account when discussing\n    46→  time commitment obligations with employers.\n    47→* In general continue to be willing to spend at least 25% of ones time working on Envoy (~1.25\n    48→  business days per week).\n    49→* We currently maintain an \"on-call\" rotation within the maintainers. Each on-call is 1 week.\n    50→  Although all maintainers are welcome to perform all of the above tasks, it is the on-call\n    51→  maintainer's responsibility to triage incoming issues/questions and marshal ongoing work\n    52→  forward. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01CcG6mdksSYerpeLC4PPaju","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Tooling docs\n     2→\n     3→## Add a Python tool\n     4→\n     5→To demonstrate adding a Python tool to the Envoy tooling lets go through the steps.\n     6→\n     7→For this example a tool with the name `mytool.py` will be added to the `/tools/sometools` directory.\n     8→\n     9→We will assume that `sometools` does not yet exist and will also need a `requirements.txt` file,\n    10→and `bazel` rule to configure the dependencies.\n    11→\n    12→In most cases of adding a tool, it is likely you will not need to create a new set of dependencies, and\n    13→you can skip to the [\"Add Python requirements\"](#add-python-requirements) section.\n    14→\n    15→We will also assume that you have `python3` and `pip` installed and working in your local environment.\n    16→\n    17→The tool *must* be runnable with `bazel`, but we can also make it runnable directly without bazel, although the user\n    18→will then have to ensure they have the necessary dependencies locally installed themselves.\n    19→\n    20→### Create the bazel boilerplate for a new set of Python requirements\n    21→\n    22→All Python requirements for Envoy tooling  must be pinned with hashes to ensure the integrity of the dependencies.\n    23→\n    24→Let's add the `bazel` boilerplate to setup a new `requirements.txt` file. This uses `rules_python`.\n    25→\n    26→Open `bazel/repositories_extra.bzl` with your editor, and find the `_python_deps` function.\n    27→\n    28→To this function, add the following bazel target:\n    29→\n    30→```starlark\n    31→    pip_install(\n    32→        name = \"sometools_pip3\",\n    33→        requirements = \"@envoy//tools/sometools:requirements.txt\",\n    34→        extra_pip_args = [\"--require-hashes\"],\n    35→    )\n    36→```\n    37→\n    38→Let's add an empty `tools/sometools/requirements.txt`:\n    39→\n    40→```console\n    41→\n    42→$ mkdir tools/sometools\n    43→$ touch tools/sometools/requirements.txt\n    44→\n    45→```\n    46→\n    47→We can now use `sometools_pip3` in the `BUILD` file for our Python tool, although we will need\n    48→some actual requirements for it to be useful.\n    49→\n    50→In order to ensure that this `requirements.txt` stays up-to-date we will also need to add an entry\n    51→in `.github/dependabot.yml`.\n    52→\n    53→This example requires the following entry:\n    54→\n    55→```yaml\n    56→  - package-ecosystem: \"pip\"\n    57→    directory: \"/tools/sometools\"\n    58→    schedule:\n    59→      interval: \"daily\"\n    60→```\n    61→\n    62→\n    63→### Add Python requirements\n    64→\n    65→For the purpose of this example, `mytool.py` will have dependencies on the `requests` and `pyyaml`\n    66→libraries.\n    67→\n    68→Check on pypi for the most recent versions.\n    69→\n    70→At the time of writing these were `2.25.1` and `5.4.1` for `requests` and `pyyaml` respectively.\n    71→\n    72→Add the pinned dependencies to the `tools/sometools/requirements.txt` file:\n    73→\n    74→```console\n    75→\n    76→$ echo pyyaml==5.4.1 >> tools/sometools/requirements.txt\n    77→$ echo requests==2.25.1 >> tools/sometools/requirements.txt\n    78→\n    79→```\n    80→\n    81→So far we have not set the hashes for the requirements.\n    82→\n    83→The easiest way to add the necessary hashes and dependencies is to use `pip-compile` from `pip-tools`.\n    84→\n    85→This will pin all dependencies *of these libraries* too.\n    86→\n    87→Run the following to update the `requirements.txt`:\n    88→\n    89→```console\n    90→\n    91→$ pip install pip-tools\n    92→$ pip-compile --generate-hashes tools/sometools/requirements.txt\n    93→\n    94→```\n    95→\n    96→### Add the Python tool\n    97→\n    98→For the purpose of this example we will add a trivial tool that dumps information\n    99→about a Python package as `yaml`\n   100→\n   101→Create a file `tools/sometools/mytool.py` with the following content:\n   102→\n   103→\n   104→```python\n   105→#!/usr/bin/env python3\n   106→\n   107→import sys\n   108→\n   109→import requests\n   110→import yaml\n   111→\n   112→\n   113→def main(*args) -> int:\n   114→    sys.stdout.write(\n   115→        yaml.dump(\n   116→            requests.get(f\"https://pypi.python.org/pypi/{args[0]}/json\").json()))\n   117→    return 0\n   118→\n   119→\n   120→if __name__ == \"__main__\":\n   121→    sys.exit(main(*sys.argv[1:]))\n   122→```\n   123→\n   124→### Create the `BUILD` file for `sometools`\n   125→\n   126→If you are adding a tool to an existing toolset you may be able skip this step -\n   127→just make sure that `load` lines are present.\n   128→\n   129→Add the following content to the file `tools/sometools/BUILD`\n   130→\n   131→```starlark\n   132→load(\"//tools/base:envoy_python.bzl\", \"envoy_py_binary\")\n   133→load(\"@sometools_pip3//:requirements.bzl\", \"requirement\")\n   134→\n   135→licenses([\"notice\"])  # Apache 2\n   136→```\n   137→\n   138→Note the loading of `requirement` from `@sometools_pip3`, and the loading of `envoy_py_binary`.\n   139→\n   140→We will use these in the next section.\n   141→\n   142→### Create the bazel target for the tool\n   143→\n   144→Add `mytool.py` as an `envoy_py_binary` to the `tools/sometools/BUILD` file.\n   145→\n   146→This will make the `mytool.py` file runnable as a `bazel` target, and will\n   147→add a test runner to ensure the file is tested.\n   148→\n   149→```starlark\n   150→envoy_py_binary(\n   151→    name = \"tools.sometools.mytool\",\n   152→    deps = [\n   153→        requirement(\"requests\"),\n   154→        requirement(\"pyyaml\"),\n   155→    ],\n   156→)\n   157→```\n   158→\n   159→Note that the `envoy_py_binary` expects the full dotted name of the module - in this case,\n   160→`tools.sometool.mytool`.\n   161→\n   162→This will create a runnable target with the name of just `mytool`.\n   163→\n   164→With this added users that have `bazel` installed can run the tool with the following command:\n   165→\n   166→```console\n   167→$ bazel run //tools/sometools:mytool PACKAGENAME\n   168→```\n   169→\n   170→\n   171→### Make the tool runnable without bazel\n   172→\n   173→If you want users to be able to run the tool directly without `bazel`, you will need\n   174→to make it executable:\n   175→\n   176→```console\n   177→$ chmod +x tools/sometools/mytool.py\n   178→```\n   179→\n   180→With this, users that have the necessary Python dependencies locally installed\n   181→can run the tool directly with the following command:\n   182→\n   183→```console\n   184→$ ./tools/sometools/mytool.py PACKAGENAME\n   185→\n   186→```\n   187→\n   188→### Add unit tests for the tool\n   189→\n   190→Envoy tooling is tested with `pytest`.\n   191→\n   192→The test runner expects a test file, in this case `tools/sometools/tests/test_mytool.py`.\n   193→\n   194→First, create the required directory if it is not present.\n   195→\n   196→```console\n   197→$ mkdir tools/sometools/tests\n   198→```\n   199→\n   200→Now add the following content to the `tools/sometools/tests/test_mytool.py` file:\n   201→\n   202→```python\n   203→from unittest.mock import patch\n   204→\n   205→from tools.sometools import mytool\n   206→\n   207→\n   208→def test_mytool_main():\n   209→    with patch(\"tools.sometools.mytool.requests.get\") as m_get:\n   210→        with patch(\"tools.sometools.mytool.yaml.dump\") as m_yaml:\n   211→            with patch(\"tools.sometools.mytool.sys.stdout.write\") as m_stdout:\n   212→                assert mytool.main(\"PACKAGENAME\") == 0\n   213→    assert (\n   214→        list(m_get.call_args)\n   215→        == [('https://pypi.python.org/pypi/PACKAGENAME/json',), {}])\n   216→    assert (\n   217→        list(m_get.return_value.json.call_args)\n   218→        == [(), {}])\n   219→    assert (\n   220→        list(m_yaml.call_args)\n   221→        == [(m_get.return_value.json.return_value,), {}])\n   222→    assert (\n   223→        list(m_stdout.call_args)\n   224→        == [(m_yaml.return_value,), {}])\n   225→```\n   226→\n   227→This example use the mock library to patch all of the method calls, and\n   228→then tests that they have been called with the expected values.\n   229→\n   230→You can run the test using the (automatically generated) `//tools/sometools:pytest_mytool` target as follows:\n   231→\n   232→```console\n   233→$ bazel run //tools/sometools:pytest_mytool\n   234→INFO: Analyzed target //tools/sometools:pytest_mytool (0 packages loaded, 0 targets configured).\n   235→INFO: Found 1 target...\n   236→Target //tools/sometools:pytest_mytool up-to-date:\n   237→  bazel-bin/tools/sometools/pytest_mytool\n   238→INFO: Elapsed time: 0.247s, Critical Path: 0.07s\n   239→INFO: 1 process: 1 internal.\n   240→INFO: Build completed successfully, 1 total action\n   241→================================ test session starts ===========================\n   242→platform linux -- python 3.8.1, pytest-6.2.3, py-1.10.0, pluggy-0.13.1 -- /usr/bin/python3\n   243→cachedir: .pytest_cache\n   244→rootdir: /root/.cache/bazel/_bazel_root/f704bab1b165ed1368cb88f9f49e7532/execroot/envoy/bazel-out/k8-fastbuild/bin/tools/sometools/pytest_mytool.runfiles/envoy, configfile: pytest.ini\n   245→plugins: cov-2.11.1\n   246→collected 1 item\n   247→\n   248→tools/sometools/tests/test_mytool.py::test_mytool_main PASSED                                         Stmts   Miss  Cover\n   252→-------------------------------------------------------------------------------\n   253→/src/workspace/envoy/tools/sometools/mytool.py                  7      0   100%\n   254→/src/workspace/envoy/tools/sometools/pytest_mytool.py           4      4     0%\n   255→/src/workspace/envoy/tools/sometools/tests/test_mytool.py      11      0   100%\n   256→-------------------------------------------------------------------------------\n   257→TOTAL                                                          22      4    82%\n   258→\n   259→\n   260→================================ 1 passed in 0.46s ============================\n   261→```\n   262→\n   263→### The `patches` pytest fixture\n   264→\n   265→When writing unit tests its not uncommon to need to patch a lot of different code.\n   266→\n   267→A `patches` fixture has been added to make this easier.\n   268→\n   269→The above test can be rewritten to make use of it as follows:\n   270→\n   271→```python\n   272→from tools.sometools import mytool\n   273→\n   274→\n   275→def test_mytool_main(patches):\n   276→    patched = patches(\n   277→        \"requests.get\",\n   278→        \"yaml.dump\",\n   279→        \"sys.stdout.write\",\n   280→        prefix=\"tools.sometools.mytool\")\n   281→\n   282→    with patched as (m_get, m_yaml, m_stdout):\n   283→        assert mytool.main(\"PACKAGENAME\") == 0\n   284→    assert (\n   285→        list(m_get.call_args)\n   286→        == [('https://pypi.python.org/pypi/PACKAGENAME/json',), {}])\n   287→    assert (\n   288→        list(m_get.return_value.json.call_args)\n   289→        == [(), {}])\n   290→    assert (\n   291→        list(m_yaml.call_args)\n   292→        == [(m_get.return_value.json.return_value,), {}])\n   293→    assert (\n   294→        list(m_stdout.call_args)\n   295→        == [(m_yaml.return_value,), {}])\n   296→\n   297→```\n   298→\n   299→### Debugging your code and tests\n   300→\n   301→You will most likely want to make use of source-level debugging when writing tests.\n   302→\n   303→Add a breakpoint anywhere in your code or tests as follows:\n   304→\n   305→```python\n   306→breakpoint()\n   307→```\n   308→\n   309→This will drop you into the Python debugger (`pdb`) at the breakpoint.\n   310→\n   311→### Using the `tools.base.runner.Runner` class\n   312→\n   313→A base class for writing tools that need to parse command line arguments has been provided.\n   314→\n   315→To make use of it in this example we will need to add the runner as a dependency to the `tools.sometools.mytool` target.\n   316→\n   317→Edit `tools/sometools/BUILD` and change the `tools.sometools.mytool` target to the following:\n   318→\n   319→```starlark\n   320→envoy_py_binary(\n   321→    name = \"tools.sometools.mytool\",\n   322→    deps = [\n   323→        \"//tools/base:runner\",\n   324→        requirement(\"requests\"),\n   325→        requirement(\"pyyaml\"),\n   326→    ],\n   327→)\n   328→```\n   329→\n   330→With this dependency in place we could rewrite the tool as follows:\n   331→\n   332→```python\n   333→#!/usr/bin/env python3\n   334→\n   335→import sys\n   336→\n   337→import requests\n   338→import yaml\n   339→\n   340→from tools.base.runner import Runner\n   341→\n   342→\n   343→class Mytool(Runner):\n   344→\n   345→    def add_arguments(self, parser):\n   346→        parser.add_argument(\"package\", help=\"Package to fetch info for\")\n   347→\n   348→    def run(self) -> int:\n   349→        sys.stdout.write(\n   350→            yaml.dump(\n   351→                requests.get(\n   352→                    f\"https://pypi.python.org/pypi/{self.args.package}/json\").json()))\n   353→        return 0\n   354→\n   355→\n   356→def main(*args) -> int:\n   357→    return Mytool(*args).run()\n   358→\n   359→\n   360→if __name__ == \"__main__\":\n   361→    sys.exit(main(*sys.argv[1:]))\n   362→```\n   363→\n   364→This will add `help` to the tool and improve the end users experience of using it.\n   365→\n   366→You can invoke the help menu using `bazel`:\n   367→\n   368→\n   369→```console\n   370→\n   371→$ bazel run //tools/sometools:mytool -- -h\n   372→...\n   373→usage: mytool.py [-h] package\n   374→\n   375→positional arguments:\n   376→  package     Package to fetch info for\n   377→\n   378→optional arguments:\n   379→  -h, --help  show this help message and exit\n   380→```\n   381→\n   382→or directly with `python`:\n   383→\n   384→```console\n   385→$ ./tools/sometools/mytool.py -h\n   386→...\n   387→```\n   388→\n   389→### Using the `tools.base.checker.Checker` class\n   390→\n   391→A base class for writing checkers (for example, linting tools) has also been provided.\n   392→\n   393→Any classes subclassing `tools.base.checker.Checker` should provide a tuple of `__class__.checks`.\n   394→\n   395→For each named check in `checks` the `Checker` will expect a method of the same name with the prefix `check_`.\n   396→\n   397→For example, setting `checks` to the tuple `(\"check1\", \"check2\")` the `Checker` will run the methods `check_check1` and `check_check2` in order.\n   398→\n   399→Let's look at an example.\n   400→\n   401→First, we need to add the bazel target.\n   402→\n   403→Edit `tools/sometools/BUILD` and add a `tools.sometools.mychecker` target with a dependency on the base `Checker`.\n   404→\n   405→```starlark\n   406→envoy_py_binary(\n   407→    name = \"tools.sometools.mychecker\",\n   408→    deps = [\n   409→        \"//tools/base:checker\",\n   410→    ],\n   411→)\n   412→```\n   413→\n   414→Next add the `MyChecker` class to `tools/sometools/mychecker.py` as follows:\n   415→\n   416→```python\n   417→#!/usr/bin/env python3\n   418→\n   419→import sys\n   420→\n   421→from tools.base.checker import Checker\n   422→\n   423→\n   424→class MyChecker(Checker):\n   425→    checks = (\"check1\", \"check2\")\n   426→\n   427→    def check_check1(self) -> None:\n   428→        # checking code for check1\n   429→        try:\n   430→            do_something()\n   431→        except NotSuchABadError:\n   432→            self.warn(\"check1\", [\"Doing something didn't work out quite as expected 8/\"])\n   433→        except ATerribleError:\n   434→            self.error(\"check1\", [\"Oh noes, something went badly wrong! 8(\"])\n   435→        else:\n   436→            self.succeed(\"check1\", [\"All good 8)\"])\n   437→\n   438→    def check_check2(self) -> None:\n   439→        # checking code for check2\n   440→        try:\n   441→            do_something_else()\n   442→        except NotSuchABadError:\n   443→            self.warn(\"check2\", [\"Doing something else didn't work out quite as expected 8/\"])\n   444→        except ATerribleError:\n   445→            self.error(\"check2\", [\"Oh noes, something else went badly wrong! 8(\"])\n   446→        else:\n   447→            self.succeed(\"check2\", [\"All good 8)\"])\n   448→\n   449→\n   450→def main(*args) -> int:\n   451→    return MyChecker(*args).run()\n   452→\n   453→\n   454→if __name__ == \"__main__\":\n   455→    sys.exit(main(*sys.argv[1:]))\n   456→```\n   457→\n   458→Just like with the `Runner` class described [above](#using-the-toolsbaserunnerrunner-class) you can\n   459→use both with and without `bazel`. To use without, you will need make it executable, and the end\n   460→user will need to have any dependencies locally installed.\n   461→\n   462→Notice in the check methods the results of the check are logged to one of `self.error`, `self.warn`,\n   463→or `self.succeed`. Each takes a `list` of outcomes. The results will be summarized to the user at the\n   464→end of all checks.\n   465→\n   466→Just like with `Runner` a help menu is automatically created, and you can add custom arguments if\n   467→required.\n   468→\n   469→Also like `Runner`, any added `Checker` classes are expected to have unit tests, and a `pytest_mychecker` bazel target\n   470→is automatically added. With the above example, the test file should be located at `tools/sometools/tests/test_mychecker.py`.\n   471→\n   472→One key difference with the `Checker` tools and its derivatives is that it expects a `path` either specified\n   473→with `--path` or as an argument. This is used as a context (for example the Envoy src directory) for\n   474→running the checks.\n   475→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware.
